Valuation for Financial Engineering   -- textbook by David C. Shimko
Preface and Chapter 1:  Valuation Methods for Financial Engineers
â€œFinancial engineeringâ€ includes:
â€¢Financial derivative security pricing, trading and risk models
â€¢Financial risk models
â€¢Financial product design and security structuring
â€¢Corporate finance, including contingent capital structure and cash 
management decisions
1

Why are financial engineers needed?
â€¢To apply the theory to solve practical problems their 
employers face based on particular circumstances .
2

Nearly every financial decision requires a valuation ; 
â€¢but old models such as Capital Asset Pricing Model (CAPM) or Price/ 
Earnings (P/E) ratios may be too simplistic for todayâ€™s complex cash flows.
Goal of the textbook and this course: 
 to teach financial engineers  (YOU) to apply valuation theory  
 to specific corporate and market circumstances .
What the general focus will / wonâ€™t  be:
â€¢Discrete -time modeling, not continuous
â€¢Simulation and flexible solutions, not closed -form solutions or formulas
3

We will approach valuation using a comprehensive simulation and benchmarking 
framework.   Benchmarks are based on traded assets or contract prices.
Example :
To value an oil refinery,
1.Build a stochastic pro -forma;
2.Simulate the cash flows;
3.Benchmark the cash flows to traded futures and option prices in energy;
4.Discount the cash flow components separately to reflect their different risks or 
benchmarks;
5.Determine a final valuation.
4

What types  of assets need to be valued?
â€¢Capital assets  = financial instruments like stocks and bonds, having contractual or 
otherwise expected cash flows;
â€¢Physical assets  = non -financial assets whose values derive from tangible cash flows 
that they produce (as opposed to intangible benefits);
â€¢Derivatives  = contracts that may require little or no initial capital investment, but may 
become significant assets or liabilities over time;
â–ªOne personâ€™s asset = another personâ€™s liability, so we must be able to value both assets 
AND liabilities;
â€¢Corporate assets and liabilities  = assets whose values are determined in part by 
choices made at corporate headquarters such as:
oCapital budgeting decisions (when and how to invest);
oCapital structure decisions (how to finance the company);
oRisk management decisions (how to select and manage the companyâ€™s risk profile).
5

Weâ€™ll use the following general approach:
â€¢Weâ€™ll focus on assets or contracts that produce cash flows with known probability 
distributions, or at least cash flows that can be stochastically defined or simulated. 
â€¢These probability distributions may be altered within defined bounds at the asset 
managerâ€™s discretion to maximize their values. 
â€¢Weâ€™ll assume the risk -free term structure is known. 
â€¢Our goal will be to value any cash flow that can be defined stochastically. 
â€¢Weâ€™ll determine risk adjustments to discount rates based on market benchmarks, 
or alternatively weâ€™ll deduct risk charges first in order to discount cash flows at the 
risk-free rate.
Donâ€™t worry, each component will be discussed in much more detail!
6

This course will proceed as follows:
â€¢Part I:  Valuation problems when discount rates (including 
any risk adjustments) are known. 
â€¢Part II:  Methods for valuing cash flows when the risk 
adjustments are unknown ahead of time.
â€¢Part III:  Valuation of corporate assets and liabilities.
7

8Asset types:
Intangible Assets  
 Capital Assets    
 Tangible Assets
â€¢Intangible assets (for example, a company brand)
â€¢Capital assets (for example, a share of stock) 
â€¢Tangible assets (for example, a company building)

9Common types of capital assets :
Share of stock = % ownership share which entitles shareholders to 
1.Any appreciation in price of the corporation (â€œcapital gainâ€)
2.Corporate payouts to shareholders (â€œdividendsâ€)
Bond = claim to
1.Interest payments (â€œcouponsâ€)
2.Any capital gains
3.Scheduled principal payments
Real estate = claim to
1.Rental payments
2.Any capital gains

11The valuation of assets can be affected by their circumstances:
â€¢Limited liability = legal protection against claims that exceed the 
value of corporate assets
â€¢Bankruptcy  = right to default on debt by surrendering assets to 
debtholders for whatever the assets are worth
â€¢Tax incentives , such as the tax deductibility of interest paid on debt 
issued by corporations, which increase net cash flows to the firm

12Therefore, in sum:
Limited liability
Bankruptcy protection
Tax incentivesïƒIncreased
Value
of Company
Shareholders never lose more than they invest, so they benefit from 
limited liability also!

13Contracts can be thought of as â€œcontingent assetsâ€
 They commit the parties to actions that may create value for all, 
 or destroy values for one party or the other, over time.
For example, 
â€¢A farmer commits to a forward contract to sell wheat in the future for $4 
per bushel, 
â€¢but the market price increases to $5, 
â€¢he faces an opportunity loss of $1 per bushel, and the value of the 
contract is negative  to himEven if contracts have no value at inception, 
they may end up destroying value for one party over time, in which 
case the contract value to that party would be negative. 

14Additional Terminology
Ask price = price offered by the seller
Bid price = price offered by the buyer
â€¢A market order to buy on an exchange will be executed at the ask price  
â€¢A market order to sell on an exchange will be executed at the bid price  
â€¢But keep in mind, the value  of an asset might differ significantly from its price . 
â€¢In addition, the public value  of an asset may differ significantly from itâ€™s 
private value . 
â€•You should only buy an asset if your private valuation is greater than the 
public valuation.
â€•You should only sell an asset if you private valuation is less than the public 
valuation.

15In sum, ITâ€™S VERY IMPORTANT TO REMEMBER THAT:
â€¢The value of an asset is NOT a simple calculation. 
â€¢It is the result of a MODEL, based on ASSUMPTIONS.

16Some useful valuation models:
1. Liquidation value
How much the highest available bidder will pay for an asset at this time
2. Replacement cost
How much it would cost to reconstruct the asset
3. Comparable value
The value of similar assets, with possible valuation adjustments for imperfect 
matches (e.g. similar properties in real estate)
4. Benchmark Valuation 
Similar to comparables , but allows a wide range of assets and contracts to be 
used to replicate the cash flows of the asset as well as possible, using 
securities and assets whose prices are known. 
5. Net present value
The current value of future cash flows and liquidation value created by an 
asset
6. Portfolio value
The increase in the value of a company as a result of the addition of the asset.
We will focus on #5 and #6 in this class.

17Key points about valuation models:
â€¢Most widely used valuation methods in accounting valuations are replacement 
cost and comparable value due to verifiability
â€¢Financial valuations tend to be more subjective and private
â€¢Benchmark valuation of Amazon stock might use portfolio of retail and tech 
stocks that best match Amazonâ€™s projected earnings
â€¢Portfolio valuation important if all parties donâ€™t value an asset in the same way

18For our purposes,
â€¢â€œAssetâ€ = anything that creates cash flows and capital appreciation/depreciation. 
â€¢â€œTotal returnâ€ = incremental net benefit produced by asset over a period of time. 
â€¢Cash flows can be positive or negative.  Positive means we receive  money (good 
guy), negative means we pay money (bad guy).
â€¢â€œCapital gainâ€ = change in asset value = appreciation  or depreciation  over time.  
(We will often refer to a negative capital gain as a â€œcapital loss.â€)

19Rate Definitions :
Present Value (PV) = Value of CFs occurring at different times in the future
Future Value (FV) = Value of CFs at a fixed future date
Time value of money = % rate of change in value of riskless CFs over time
Rate of return (ROR)  = Growth rate of asset or portfolio over time (expected or actual)
Capital gain rate = change in price, note it is only one component of total return
Total return (ROR) = capital gains AND cash payments, m/b expressed in $ or as a %
Growth rate = change in CFs over time (â‰  ROR)
Default rate = likelihood CFs wonâ€™t materialize due to bankruptcy or other failure

â€¢Then, it actually paid $0.50 in dividends and appreciated by $10 in value. 
â€¢Then we could say any and all of the following:

â€¢The expected one-year capital gain was ????? one year ago 
â€¢The expected dividend was ????? one year ago
â€¢The realized one-year total dollar return was ?????
â€¢The realized one-year capital gain was ?????
â€¢The realized one-year dividend was ?????
â€¢Note however that all of these can be answered in dollars or percentages!Answer 
these 
questions 
in dollars.Prices, Returns, Expectations and Realizations

Required and Expected Returns
21â€¢Why do investors require a minimum level of 
returns?
â€¢Foregone time value
â€¢For giving up cash, investors must be 
compensated the interest (r) they 
would have earned in a risk -free 
investment of equal initial value (V0)
â€¢Incurred risk cost
â€¢For taking the risk of loss, investors 
must earn additional compensation
â€¢This is typically computed by 
assuming a risk measure ( ï³) and 
multiplying by a cost per unit risk (k)
â€¢In sum, investors require compensation for lost 
time value and incremental risk cost.ï‚—What returns do investors expect to earn from 
an investment over the next time period?
ï‚¡Capital gains
ïƒ·This is the change in the value of the 
investment, (V1-V0), whether they sell it or 
not
ï‚¡Cash flows
ïƒ·This refers to cash income (C) earned from 
the investment, e.g. stock dividends, bond 
coupons or other payments
ï‚—In sum, investors earn returns through capital 
gains or cash flow, and only care about the sum.

This key relationship determines pricing:
22â€¢What is the required total dollar return on an asset (two components)?
Return on Cash + Compensation for Risk
â€¢What is the expected dollar return on an asset (two components)?
Expected Gains + Expected Cash Flows
â€¢What is the relationship between required and expected return for a buyer of an asset?
Required Return â‰¤Expected Return
â€¢What is the relationship between required and expected return for a seller of an asset?
Required Return â‰¥Expected Return
â€¢How do you reconcile these two equations?
Required Return = Expected Return

23In sum,
Required Return  = Return on Cash + Compensation for Risk 
Expected Return  = Expected Gains + Expected Cash Flows
A buyer demands:     Required Return â‰¤ Expected Return
A seller demands:     Required Return â‰¥ Expected Return
To reach equilibrium:  Required Return = Expected Return

The GVE, or General Valuation Equation
24â€¢GVE:  Required return = Expected return
â€¢Required return = Compensation for time plus compensation  for risk
â€¢Expected return = Expected capital gains plus cash flows
â€¢Notation
â€¢r = risk -free interest rate
â€¢C1= cash flow at time 1
â€¢V0= valuation at time 0
â€¢V1= valuation at time 1
â€¢ï³= standard deviation (an example of a risk measure, to be explained later)
â€¢k = compensation per unit risk
â€¢Can you write the GVE using this notation?

Key takeaway :  Because the components of the GVE are functions of the value, we can solve for the 
value V0that makes the equation hold

26Goal is to determine V0
Required Return = time value of money + compensation for risk
  = risk -free opportunity cost of funds + compensation for risk
  = r V0 + 0
Expected Return = expected capital gain (CG) + expected cash flow (CF)
  = (0 - V0) + C
or  = (C - V0) + 0  (i.e., there are two ways of characterizing the transaction)
Required Retirn  = Expected Return
  r V0  + 0 = (C - V0) + 0
  r V0  = C - V0
   (1+r) V0  = C 
  V0 = C/(1+r)

When returns are undefined
27â€¢If you bought a stock for $50 and sold for $60, what was the return?
â€¢DOLLARS ___________ PERCENT _____________
â€¢A short sale requires the following sequence:
â€¢Borrow the stock
â€¢Sell it
â€¢Buy it back later, hopefully at a lower price (thereby earning a profit)
â€¢Return the stock to the owner
â€¢If you sold the stock short at $50 and later repurchased it for $40, 
what was the return?
â€¢DOLLARS ___________ PERCENT _____________
â€¢What is the percentage return on the short sale of a stock you sold at $50 and bought back at $40?
â€¢Impossible to compute!
â€¢We have the same problem with futures trades and short option trades
This illustrates the necessity of being able to measure returns and risk in dollars, not percentages!

28Another example:
Forward contract to buy a barrel of oil in one year at $50
    (I.e., the one -year forward price is $50)
Suppose the actual price after one year is $51
    Profit = $1
    But % return = $1 / $0   Undefined!
Conclusion :
Can use % returns for assets that are purchased and paid for in cash and have limited 
liability protection, such as long  positions in stocks and bonds
For short  positions and forward contracts, must analyze in terms of â€œdollarizedâ€   
returns, not % returns

When returns are undefined, contâ€™d
29An alternative way of analyzing short sales:
â€¢Same example:  Assume you sold the stock short at $50 and later repurchased it for $40, 
What was the dollar return and percent return?
â€¢For this short sale, one can reason as follows:
â€¢Initial investment = -$50 (because you receive this amount by borrowing the stock)
â€¢Ending cash flow = -$40 (because you pay this amount by returning the stock to the owner)
â€¢Hence the dollar return = ( -$40) â€“(-$50) = $10 (we already knew this intuitively)
â€¢And the percentage return = dollar return/initial investment = ($10)/( -$50) = -20%
â€¢Note that from this perspective, shorting converts a negative percentage return into a positive dollar 
return, because the original investment is negative:
â€¢Dollar return = %return x initial investment = ( -20%) x ( -$50) = $10
â€¢This alternative treatment of short transactions, while correct and sometimes useful, might seem a 
little strange because thereâ€™s no initial investment like there is in long transactions.
â€¢Also note this alternative treatment wouldnâ€™t work (i.e., still wouldnâ€™t produce a meaningful 
percentage return) for a forward transaction/contract .

30Power and generality of the Generalized Valuation Equation :
GVE can be used to value:
â€¢Stocks and bonds (capital assets)
â€¢Annuities and perpetuities
â€¢Structured debt products
â€¢Risky cash flows
â€¢Futures and forward contracts
â€¢Options and real options
â€¢Corporate assets and liabilities
Special cases of GVE:
â€¢Benchmarking models for equity
â€¢Modigliani -Miller proposition
â€¢Capital Asset Pricing Model (CAPM)
â€¢Black Scholes Merton (BSM) option pricing differential equation
Stay tuned!!!

31A little more on valuing liabilities â€¦. Insurance interlude:
â€œValuationâ€ for insurance companies frequently means â€œcalculation of 
reserves.â€
Reserve = â€œsomething kept back or saved for future useâ€
 = PV future benefits & expenses â€“ PV future premiums

32Purposes of reserves:
1.To maintain company solvency (â€œstatutory or solvency 
reservesâ€)
2.To help determine company earnings for financial reporting 
(â€œearnings or GAAP reservesâ€)
3.To help determine company taxable earnings (â€œtax reservesâ€)

33It is necessary for insurance companies to hold reserves if premiums 
exceed benefits in early years but not in later years
Extremely Simplistic Hypothetical Example â€“ 3 Year Term Policy

Premium Benefits Reserve

34At any given point in time, reserves can be calculated in two ways:
We can look backwards and compare the accumulated value of premiums 
received minus the accumulated value of benefits and expenses paid out;
We can look forwards and compare the present value of benefits and expenses 
we expect to pay out in the future to the present values of premiums we expect 
to receive.
Under certain conditions, the two amounts will be equal.
Actual reserve methods must comply with insurance laws, regulations, 
accounting & tax rules.

35Key takeaways from insurance interlude :
Valuation of liabilities and of assets are both very important.
Present values and future values are focused on a particular point in time
In order to  compare varying cash flows, one discounts or accumulates them to the 
same point in time
Itâ€™s extremely important to be clear about the specific point in time you are using 
and not lose sight of it .

â€¢PVA (C, n, r, Vn, s) = PV of flat annuity of n equally spaced payments of C, discount rate = r.

â€¢PVGA (C1, n, r, g, Vn, s) = PV of annuity whose CFs grow at rate g,  C1 = first payment
â€¢PVLGP (a, b, r, s) = PV of linearly growing perpetuity, b = fixed dollar increase in CFs. CFt = 
a + bt, first payment CFs = a + bs. 
â€¢PVLGA (a, b, n, r, s) = PV of linearly growing annuity, n = # of payments, b = fixed dollar 
increase in CFs.   CF = a + bt at time t, first payment = a + bs, last payment a + bn. 
â€¢PVMRA (C1, Ò¡, Âµ, n,r,s) = PV of mean -reverting annuity, Âµ =  long -run mean cash flow, Ò¡ = 
mean -reverting speed

18Valuing an Annuity
Recall that we introduced the notation from the text PVA (C, n, r, Vn, s)
PVA (C, n, r, Vn, s) = PV of flat annuity of n equally spaced payments of C, discount rate = 
r. Vn = maturity value in addition to last payment of C (if any), s = starting date of the 
first payment of C.

maturity value Vn, the symbol for the present value would be PVA (C, n, r, 0, 1)

A Simple Paradigm for Common Cash Flow Patterns
â€¢Many cash flow patterns can be modeled as a 
growing annuity with a final payment
â€¢Once you know 5 of the 6 inputs, the missing 
value can be determined
â€¢Some details:
â€¢Make sure the period is consistent with 
the parameter (n, r)
â€¢The first payment occurs in one period
â€¢The PV is often expressed as a negative 
number, as if it is an investment that 
receives positive cash flows
â€¢The FV is the amount received in addition 
to the final payment
â€¢g is the percentage growth rate in the cash 
flow per period
â€¢On some financial calculators, r 
(sometimes denoted by i) is expressed as a 
% rather than a decimal

23Example: 
â€¢Your client has been in an automobile accident and was awarded $50,000 per 
year for 25 years as a settlement, starting in one year.

27Valuing a Simplified Bond
The issuer of a simplified bond receives a loan of â€œFâ€ in return for promising to
â€¢make annual interest payments of amount â€œCâ€;
â€¢repay â€œFâ€ at the end of the n -year term
F is sometimes called the face value, maturity value or principal of the bond.
The present value (PV) is the sum of an n -year annuity and the final payment:
0nnV Ca Fv=+
1n
n vC Fvrïƒ¦ïƒ¶âˆ’=+ïƒ§ïƒ·ïƒ¨ïƒ¸
where
1
1vr=+

28Example:
Suppose the coupon rate is 5% (this is the rate of interest that the bond issuer promises to pay).
If the principal is $1,000, the coupon would be 5% x $1,000 = $50.
What is the PV of the bond if the term is 10 years and the discount rate is 4%, 5% or 6%?
At 4%, V0 = $1,081.11   At 5%, V0 = $1,000.00   At 6%, V0 =    $926.40
From this we see the following implications of the coupon rate versus the discount rate:
â€¢coupon rate greater than discount rate ïƒ value greater than face amount;
â€¢coupon rate equal to discount rate ïƒ value equal face amount;
â€¢coupon rate less than discount rate ïƒ value less than face amount.
Conversely, we can see the following implications of the value versus the face amount:
â€¢value less than face amount ïƒ discount rate greater than coupon rate;
â€¢value equal to face amount ïƒ discount rate equal to coupon rate;
â€¢value greater than face amount ïƒ discount rate less than coupon rate.

29Itâ€™s easy to see that this is true in general.  For example, assume the coupon rate = the 
discount rate r;
01n
n vV C Fvrïƒ¦ïƒ¶âˆ’=+ïƒ§ïƒ·ïƒ¨ïƒ¸
()1n
n vrF Fvrïƒ¦ïƒ¶âˆ’=+ïƒ§ïƒ·ïƒ¨ïƒ¸
()() 1nnF v Fv= âˆ’ +
F=

30Terminology:
â€¢If you are an investor who purchases a bond at its face value F, you are said to 
be buying the bond â€œ at par value â€ or â€œ at par, â€   
â€¢If you pay more than the face value you are said to be buying the bond â€œ at a 
premium ,â€ and your yield will be less than the coupon rate .  
â€¢If you pay less than the face value you are said to be be buying the bond â€œ at a 
discount ,â€ and your yield will be more than the coupon rate .  
â€¢The market price of a bond may be above or below par, depending on factors 
such as the general level of interest rates that investors can earn on other 
investments and the bond issuerâ€™s credit status.

31In addition, looking more closely at the changes in the present value of the bond if 
the discount rate is 4%, 5% or 6%, we see the following pattern as the discount rate 
increases:
At 4%, V0 = $1,081.11
At 5%, V0 = $1,000.00;  ï„ = -$81.11
At 6%, V0 =    $926.40;  ï„ = -$73.60
This shows that bond prices are â€œconvex in the discount rate.â€  
That is, they decrease in value at a decreasing rate as interest rates rise.

34Zero Coupon Bonds (ZCBs)
ZCBs are bonds that pay no coupons.  
Examples are U.S. government T -bills and STRIPs.
If 
â€¢t = time to maturity 
â€¢r(t) = discount rate for maturity at time t
â€¢F = face value
then
0(1 )tFVr=+
Why are ZCBs important  ??
Because we can use ZCBs to value CFs with different discount rates for different maturities!

39Valuing a Growing Annuity
PVGA (C1, n, r, g, Vn, s) = PV of annuity whose first payment is C1, n equally spaced 
payments, discount rate = r. g = growth rate, Vn = maturity value in addition to last 
payment (if any), s = starting date of the first payment.   Typically, Vn = 0 and s = 1, i.e., 
we will value PVGA (C1, n, r, g, 0, 1).

53Now that weâ€™ve covered the perpetuity, we can discuss the corresponding linearly 
growing annuity:
PVLGA (a, b, n, r, s) = PV of linearly growing annuity, n = # of payments, b = fixed dollar 
increase in CFs.   CF = a + bt at time t, first payment = a + bs, last payment a + bn. 
PVLGA (a, b, n, r, s) can then be valued as the difference between two perpetuities, with 
at least one of them deferred. 
For example, if the annuity payment starts at time 1 and ends at n, we have 
payment                 a+b           a+2b          a+3b         â€¦              a+nb       a+(n+1) b â€¦ etc. 
 
time 0 1 2 3 â€¦ n n+1    â€¦  etc. 
PVLGA (a, b, n, r, s) = PVLGP (a, b, r, 1) âˆ’ PVLGP (a, b, r, n+1)
Important note:  As with geometrically growing annuities, it may be helpful to define
    C0 = C1 â€“ b = (a + b) â€“ b = a
so that the payment at time 1 equals the desired C1.

54Example: A business expects to bring in one client per year for the next 15 years. If 
each client is worth $50,000 per year in perpetuity, and the discount rate is 12%, what 
is the value of this program? 
First draw a picture of the cash flows you are trying to value!
payment                 50K          2x50K          3x50K          â€¦          15x50K        15x50K  â€¦ etc.

56Advanced Annuities:  Mean Reversion
In some markets, prices follow mean -reverting patterns
â€¢Interest rates, commodities, foreign exchange
â€¢Mean -reverting prices exhibit exponential decay to a long -run mean value ( ğœ‡)
â€¢This means if the spot price today is S0, then the price next period satisfies the 
following equation:
o (S1 - ğœ‡ ) = (1 â€“ ğœ…) (S0 â€“ ğœ‡)
â€¢More generally, the spot price at time t satisfies
o (St - ğœ‡ ) = (1 â€“ ğœ…)t (S0 â€“ ğœ‡)
oğœ… is the speed of mean reversion of St to ğœ‡, i.e. the percentage of the distance in 
the direction of the long run mean
â€¢Mean reversion implies that low prices will be expected to rise, and high prices will be 
expected to fall in the future
â€¢The term captures exponential decay to the long -run mean

57
â€¢Rearranging terms, 
oSt  = ğœ‡  +  (1 â€“ ğœ…)t (S0 â€“ ğœ‡)
â€¢This implies that the spot prices can be looked at as the sum of 
oa fixed cash flow ğœ‡, plus
oan exponentially growing cash flow with starting value C1  = (1 â€“ ğœ…)1 (S0 â€“ ğœ‡), 
growth rate â€“ ğœ…, and Ct+1 = (1 â€“ ğœ…)Ct 
â€¢Recall the symbol PVMRA (C1, Ò¡, Âµ, n,r,s) = PV of mean -reverting annuity, Âµ =  long -
run mean cash flow, Ò¡ = mean -reverting speed 
â€¢Therefore, PVMRA (C1, Ò¡, Âµ,n,r,1) = PVA (Âµ, n, r, 0, 1) + PVGA (C1, n, r, â€“ ğœ…, 0, 1)

58Example:

â€¢The annual speed of mean reversion is 10%, and the discount rate is 5%.
â€¢What is the present value of 20 annual payments starting one year from now,

â€¦
S20 = (1 - .10)20(3.50 â€“ 5) + 5 = 4.81764

Simplifying assumptions that we made in Chapter 2:

23Debt instrument payment frequencies:
ï‚·Monthly â€“consumer loans
ï‚·Quarterly (i.e., every 3 months) â€“some corporate bonds
ï‚·Semiannually (i.e., every 6 months)  â€“some corporate bonds and all US 
government bonds
4Converting simple annual rates to periodic rates
Default:  r = simple annual (risk-free) interest rate
Periodic rate:
â€¢m = number of discounting periods per year
â€¢Periodic rate = simple annual rate / m
â€œSimpleâ€:  To get the annual rate you â€œsimplyâ€ multiply the periodic rate by the 
number of periods (there is no compounding), and vice-versa
â€œCompoundingâ€:  Interest being paid on interest.
Effective annual rate: The interest rate you actually earn or pay in a year when 
interest is compounded more than once per year.
â€¢Note:  The EAR is useful in comparing the yields on instruments with different 
payment frequencies.3
4

2/25/2025
35Examples:
If the simple annual rate (SAR) of 8% is compounded quarterly; what is the 
effective annual rate (EAR)?
ï‚·â€œsimple annualâ€ means the rate paid in each quarter is 8%/4 = 2%
ï‚·If interest is compounded quarterly, the accumulated value of $1 at the end 
of the year will be $1 x (1.02)4= 1.082432160
ï‚·This implies that the EAR is 8.24%
If the EAR = 12.55% is payable quarterly, what is the SAR?  Reverse the steps:
ï‚·The accumulated value of $1 at the end of the year is $1 x (1.1255)
ï‚·The accumulation factor per quarter is therefore (1.1255)1/4= 1.029997984
ï‚·This means that the rate paid in each quarter (SAR/4) is 2.9997984%
ï‚·Hence SAR = 4 x 2.9997984% = 12.00%

917Payment Frequency and Discounting Frequency
Key point:  When you encounter problems with payment frequency â‰ discounting 
frequency, first find the discount rate for the payment frequency!
For example, if the interest rate on a consumer loan is quoted on a simple annual 
basis, but the payments are made on a monthly basis, first divide the simple annual 
rate by 12.

11Inflation
21â€¢Just like interest rates change, so do inflation rates
â€¢Inflation implies the spending power of our money 
shrinks (proportionately) as inflation increases
â€¢But it is not always bad!
â€¢Therefore money can be described in two ways:
â€¢Nominal dollars:  The actual amount of dollars 
we have
â€¢Real dollars:  What those dollars are worth in 
spending power terms, usually in reference to 
a prior year
â€¢The return on money is also expressed in two 
ways:
â€¢Nominal return:  Contractual return or actual 
return measured in currency
â€¢Real return:  Adjusted downward for inflationï‚—Problem:
ï‚¡Suppose I receive a fixed wage 
contract
ï‚¡Although I know the payment, I 
donâ€™t know future spending power
ï‚¡I could get a wage contract adjusted 
for inflation 
ïƒ·This is called a COLA clause, or 
cost of living adjustment
ïƒ·But inflation changes
ï‚¡How can I value this contract?

standardized â€œbasketâ€ of 
consumer goods, meant to 
represent changes in overall 
price levels.

1631Key Takeaway:
â€¢Always discount nominal CFs at nominal interest rates !
â€¢Always discount real CFs at real interest rates !
Also important:
â€¢Even if future inflation rates are unknown, it may be reasonable in 
some situations (such as the COLA annuity) to assume that the 
nominal CFs grow with inflation.
â€¢In this case the real CFs will be the same in every year and can be 
valued as a level annuity at the estimated real interest rate ï².

1733Taxes
Rule of thumb:  
ï‚·Always discount pre-tax CFs at pre-tax interest rates
ï‚·Always discount after-tax CFs at after-tax interest rates
Normally, these calculations will yield the same result if 
ï‚·the capital gains (CG) tax rate = ordinary income (OI) tax rate = ï´(Greek 
symbol â€œtauâ€)
In this case, if r = pre-tax interest rate and rAT= after-tax interest rate,
ï‚·rAT= (1 -ï´) r

2141Variable Interest Rates
Spot Rates
â€¢For t>0, define the t-year spot rate as follows:
rst= effective annualinterest rate earned by money invested now for a period of t years.  
â€¢Spot rates are sometimes called zero coupon rates.
â€¢Spot rates are quoted for U.S. Treasury securities or other default-free zero-coupon securities.
â€¢Treasury bills, which have maturities of 1 year or less, are zero-coupon bonds (ZCBs).
â€¢Treasury notes, which have maturities of more than 1 year but less than 10 years, and Treasury 
bonds, which have maturities of more than 10 years, pay semiannual coupons.
â€¢Hence, if you want spot rates based on U.S. Treasuries for terms of longer than one year, they 
can only be determined by removing (â€œstripping offâ€) the coupons. 
â€¢The coupon bond is decomposed into a series of ZCBs with terms increasing by 6 months each.
â€¢The resulting ZCBs are called STRIPS and actual market yields are available.
42Simplified annual example:
A one-year ZCB has price of $950 and matures at $1,000.
A two-year bond has price of $975, pays annual coupons of $50, 
and matures at $1,000.
Calculate the spot rates rs1and rs2.
First approach:  (brute force method):  
First calculate rs1:

2/25/2025
2243Second approach:  Decompose (â€œstripâ€) the two-year coupon bond with a price of $975 into two ZCBs:
ï‚·One-year ZCB paying $50
ï‚·Two-year ZCB paying $1,050
What is the price of the one-year $50 ZCB?
We were given that a one-year ZCB maturing at $1,000 has a price of $950.  
Therefore, the price of a one-year ZCB maturing at $50 must be (50/1,000) = 5% of $950, or $47.50.
What is the price â€œPâ€ of the $1,050 two-year ZCB?
Two-year coupon bond = (One-year ZCB paying $50) + (Two-year ZCB paying $1,050)
Of course, one can generalize this 
process to bonds with terms of n > 2 
years.  The general recursive process 
of solving for rstgiven rs1, rs2, â€¦, rs(t-1)
is sometimes referred to as 
â€œbootstrapping.â€975 47.50 P ï€½ ï€«

Term Structure and ZCBs
44â€¢The term structure plots the relationship between the maturity (term) of a 
bond and its yield to maturity
â€¢All bonds plotted are in the same class
â€¢Examples:  U.S. Government, Municipal, Corporate AAA
â€¢Generally upward-sloping
â€¢Downward-sloping (inverted) yield curve believed to be a forecast of 
recession
â€¢In this class, we will focus on U.S. government ZCBs
â€¢ZCB = Zero coupon bond
â€¢Any other fixed rate bond can be constructed and therefore priced from ZCBs
â€¢Easy market references for risk-free ZCBs exist in the U.S. and most 
developed economies
â€¢T-Bill (treasury bill)
â€¢U.S. Government STRIPS
â€¢Simplify present value calculations
â€¢Each discount rate is known for a particular maturity
â€¢Corresponds to cash flow at that maturity
â€¢Easier to compute forward rates

Â© 2020 David C. Shimko. All rights reserved.What is the â€œForward Rateâ€?
An interest rate implicitly â€œlocked inâ€ in the future by buying a bond today.
Can think of it as an â€œexpected,â€ â€œestimated,â€ â€œimplied,â€ or â€œbest guessâ€ future spot rate
What conditions does the forward rate need to satisfy (discrete case)?
Note that the average of the 1-year spot rate and the forward rate is about 2.2%;
In other words, 2.0% + 2.4004% ï‚»2.2%
Using continuous discounting, the forward rate is exactly 2.4%.

This shows more generally that the annual n-year spot rate is the (exact or 
approximate) average of the one-year forward rates .  
Note:  The same caveat as before with respect to the two-year average in the discrete 
case applies to the n-year average in the discrete case.  
That is, if in the discrete case we measure the compound n-year average rather than the 
arithmetic n-year average, the n-year spot rate is exactlyequal to the compound average 
of the one-year forward rates. 
50If spot rates are increasing with duration, the forward rates must therefore rise even faster.  
There is a numerical example in the text.
Intuitively, the n-year spot rate equals the average of the successive one-year forward rates. 
But the first one-year forward rate equals the first one-year spot rate, which is less than the n-
year spot rate.  Thus the one-year forward rates are starting at a lower point than the n-year 
spot rate, and hence they must increase faster in order to average to the n-year spot rate.

at future time t for a term extending from future time t to future time u.

may be able to make a profit (but only if your prediction is correct).

52â€¢Spot interest rates
â€¢Expected future spot rates
â€¢Inflation
â€¢Risk premium â€“depending on market 
demand and supply of debt
â€¢Expectations hypothesis â€“Forward rates 
equal expected future spot rates
â€¢Liquidity premium hypothesis â€“Forward 
rates exceed expected future spot rates 
because
â€¢Issuers want to naturally borrow 
longer term
â€¢Investors naturally want to lend 
shorter term

56Valuing Single Variable Cash Flows
What if we need to value a single future cash flow that is a function of the future spot 
interest rate but isnâ€™t part of a floating rate bond?  (For example, we might need to value a 
company with earnings that fluctuate with interest rates.)
In this case, we canâ€™t use the GVE to simply cancel out the unknown spot interest rate r1(or 
more generally, the unknown spot interest rate rt).  But we can use the theoretical or 
implied forward rate to estimate the future spot rate.  
For example, if the actual CF at time t equals rtF, we can model this as ft-1,tF using the 
forward rate information embedded in the spot rate yield curve:

Currency Terms
62â€¢Spot exchange rate â€“The price of foreign currency paid today
â€¢Direct quote: Units of foreign currency per USD
â€¢Indirect quote:  USD per unit of foreign currency (e.g. GBP, EUR)
â€¢Forward exchange rate â€“The price agreed today of foreign currency received 
and paid for in the future
â€¢Currency swap â€“An agreement to exchange currency in the future
â€¢Domestic, offshore and foreign interest rates
â€¢All risk-free rates are different
â€¢Eurodollars
â€¢Dollar-denominated fixed income instruments traded outside the U.S.
â€¢Purchasing Power Parity (PPP)
â€¢The theory that exchange rates are set so that equal value currency 
combinations purchase the same basket of real goods in different 
countries 
â€¢Interest Rate Parity (IRP) or covered interest arbitrage
â€¢The relationship between spot prices and forward prices of two 
currencies
â€¢Arbitrage
â€¢Trading to make a risk-free profit from price discrepancies occurring in

sometimes referred to as the â€œexpected loss rate.â€

11Valuation for Financial Engineering  --textbook by David C. Shimko
Chapter 4:  Bonds
ï‚·Once you know the yield to maturity, the coupon accrual conventions, the quoting 
conventions, applicable tax treatment, liquidity, inflation rate, and anything else that 
affects the value of the bond, you can value a bond.
ï‚·For many bonds, however, the market price is a better indicator of value than a 
theoretical model. 
ï‚·Hence, bond prices can be used to infer parameters that might be unknown or 
difficult to estimate.
ï‚·In Chapter 3, we learned how to determine bond forward rates and currency 
forward prices from the observed ZCB term structure.
ï‚·In Chapter 4, weâ€™ll see how to determine implied real rates of interest, inflation 
rates, tax rates, default rates and estimates of loss upon default for corporate bonds
ï‚·Weâ€™ll start with U.S. government bonds, then move to corporate bonds.
Govâ€™t Bonds â€“â€œRisk Freeâ€
â€¢The most basic  debt instruments are 
developed country debt
â€¢The assumption behind the pricing is 
that these securities are not subject 
to any appreciable default risk
â€¢In reality, implicit default risk 
premiums are apparent when you 
look at the rates of the various 
countries in the EU (all of whom 
issue EUR denominated debt)
â€¢The Government Debt market is huge. 
In 2020, the US Debt is on the order 
of $22 Trillion USDUS Government Debt
Source: US Treasury2

3/11/2025
2â€œFederal Debt and the Statutory Limit, February 2023
â€¢The debt limitâ€”commonly called the debt ceilingâ€”is the maximum 
amount of debt that the Department of the Treasury can issue to the public 
or to other federal agencies. 
â€¢The amount is set by law and has been increased or suspended over the 
years to allow for the additional borrowing needed to finance the 
governmentâ€™s operations. 
â€¢On December 16, 2021, lawmakers raised the debt limit by $2.5 trillion to a 
total of $31.4 trillion. 
â€¢On January 19, 2023, that limit was reached, and the Treasury announced a 
â€œdebt issuance suspension periodâ€ during which, under current law, it can 
take well-established â€œextraordinary measuresâ€ to borrow additional funds 
without breaching the debt ceiling.â€
Source:  Congressional Budget Office, www.cbo.gov/publication/58945
3
The yield curve
4â€¢Yield to maturity (YTM)
â€¢This is the yield you will earn if you buy the bond 
at the offer price and hold it to maturity
â€¢Assumes same compounding convention as the 
bond 
â€¢(i.e. semiannual yield x 2 or quarterly yield x 4)
â€¢Yield curve definition
â€¢Also called the term structure of interest rates
â€¢For a given bond category, the yield curve shows 
how YTM depends on time
â€¢Yield curve shape
â€¢Generally upward-sloping
â€¢U.S. Treasury Bond sample shownDirection this month

center/interest-rates/pages/textview.aspx?data=yield

35U.S. Government Bonds
In Chapter 4 weâ€™ll discuss the following five types of U.S. government bonds:
ï‚·Treasury bills are ZCBs with less than a year to maturity. 
ï‚·Treasury notes andtreasury bonds are fixed-rate bonds:
oTreasury notes are typically issued with 5 years or less to maturity
oTreasury bonds are issued with up to 30-year maturities
ï‚·FRNs are 2-year floating rate notes with variable interest rates
ï‚·TIPS are designed to protect against unknown inflation rates
6Basic tax treatment of interest income for U.S. government bonds:
ï‚·Interest income is taxable at the federal level
ï‚·Interest income is tax-free at the state and local levels
ï‚·Principal increases for TIPS are treated like interest income5

ï‚·T-bills are ZCBs with < 1 year to maturity
ï‚·The maturity value = $10,000
The challenge in using T-bills is a legacy (i.e., â€œold-fashionedâ€ or â€œout of dateâ€) quote system.

18Idealized U.S. Government Coupon Bond
U.S. government notes and bonds have 
ï‚·semi-annual coupons = 1,000(c/2), where c = annual coupon rate;
ï‚·face value = $1,000 = maturity value
Simplifying â€œidealizedâ€ assumptions:
1. The valuation date is just after (i.e., immediately after) a coupon is paid 
2. The coupons are evenly spaced at 6 month intervals 
3. The annual yield is based on the â€œannual percentage rateâ€ (APR) of the semiannual yield 
4. Bonds are priced in decimals 
In reality, 
1. the price between coupon payment dates = quoted price + accrued interest since previous 
coupon date;
2. coupons are about 6 months apart, but the number of days between coupon payments varies by 
a few days; 
3. there are many ways to compute yield, this is just the simplest; and 
4. bonds may be priced in 32nds, not always in decimals.17

ï‚·Also called the Bond Equivalent Yield (BEY).

General steps for calculating the price or bond value using financial calculator or Excel:
ï‚·Clear financial memories if applicable
ï‚·Set the payment equal to 1000c/2
ï‚·Set the future value (value in addition to the last payment) equal to 1000
ï‚·Set the discount rate equal to the yield divided by 2 (some calculators use whole 
number yields, and others use decimal)
ï‚·Set n equal to the number of coupon payments (not the number of years!! )
ï‚·Press PV key to compute present value
20Example:  6% coupon 10-year bond with a 5% YTM.
ï‚·Set the payment equal to 1000(.06/2) = 30
ï‚·Set the future value equal to 1000
ï‚·Set the discount rate equal to the yield 5% divided by 2 = 2.5%
ï‚·Set n equal to the number of coupon payments = 2 x 10 = 20

1121Review of terminology:
If the coupon rate > YTM, the bond trades at a premium
If the coupon rate < YTM, the bond trades at a discount
If the coupon rate = YTM, the bond trades at par

1427Bootstrapping without STRIPS
ï‚·STRIPS data was widely reported in the financial press prior to 2019, but has 
become more difficult to obtain. 
ï‚·Also, financial engineers working in banks will want to use all available bond 
pricing data to get the most accurate ZCB curve possible. 
ï‚·Fortunately, a slight modification of the method described in the last section 
works well even with coupon bonds.
28Bootstrapping without STRIPS, continued
With coupon-paying bonds, the procedure is as follows:
1. Find the exact coupon dates (t) and payment amounts Ctfor each payment date
2. Use the same function as in the preceding section to model the ZCB discount rate 
function y(t):
ï€¨ï€© ï€¨ï€©5 2
1 2 3 4 6 7 loga ty t a a t a t a e a t a ï¥ ï€½ ï€« ï€« ï€« ï€« ï€­ ï€«
3. The model bond price is the present value of the payment amounts Ctfor each 
payment date using y(t) and continuous compounding:
ï€¨ï€©Model Bond Price y t t
tC eï€­ï€½ïƒ¥
4. The squared deviation is the square of the difference between the observed 
(i.e., the model) prices and the market (i.e., actual) prices of the coupon 
bonds in the bond pricing data.27
28

3/11/2025
1529Bootstrapping without STRIPS, continued
ï‚·Key point:  In the preceding sectionwe estimated the ZCB yield curve y(t) directlyusing 
STRIPS ZCB data.  We were able to compute and minimize squared deviations between 
omodel yieldsy(t) and 
oactual market yieldsof STRIPS ZCBs, which we could get directly from quotes.  
ï‚·In this sectionwe are estimating the ZCB yield curve y(t) indirectlyusing coupon bond 
data, not STRIPS ZCB data.   Hypothetical ZCB yields corresponding to coupon bond 
payments arenâ€™t availablefrom coupon bond data, but market prices of the coupon bonds 
are available.  Hence in this section, we compute squared deviations between 
omodel bond prices(corresponding to the above model yields y(t)) and 
oactual market bond prices, which we can get directly from quotes.
5. Make sure the market prices include accrued interest (dirty prices)
6. Choose the â€œaâ€ parameters to minimize the sum of squared deviations for all available risk-
free bonds.
This procedure provides a ZCB discount rate curve using continuous compounding, and can 
therefore be used to value any risk-free at cash flows at known times.

32Simplified Example: 
5 year TIPS bond, 2% coupon, Initial Principal = $1,000
Year 1: 2 payments of $10 (2%*$1,000/2)
CPI increases by 5% => New Principal = $1,050
Year 2: 2 payments of 2%*$1,050/2 = $10.50
CPI flat => Principal Remains same
Year 3: 2 payments of $10.50
CPI decreases by 3% => New Principal = $1,018.50
Year 4: 2 payments of 2%*$1,018.50/2 = $10.19
CPI decreases by 4% => New Principal =$977.76
Year 5: 2 payments of 2%*$997.76/2 = $9.98
Principal of $X returned
Q: What is â€œXâ€ in year 5? 
Q: What are the tax implications of these cash flows?$1000
Interest payments & Increases in principal (here = 0) subject to 
Federal but not state or local taxes

3/11/2025
1733TIPS and Inflation
TIPS stands for Treasury Inflation-Protected Securities
First issued by U.S. government in 1997.
Provide protection against inflation, especially for pension funds and long-term 
investors.
Adjusted Principal = Original Principal x (CPIt/CPI0)
Maturity Value = max (Original Principal, Adjusted Principal)   (embedded option)
Semiannual interest = (Fixed Coupon Rate) x Adjusted Principal
Note:  Unlike the maturity value, the interest payments can fall with deflation (no 
guaranteed floor)
34Additional notes:
TIPS arenâ€™t a complete inflation hedge because not only coupons but also increases 
in principal are subject to federal taxation (but neither is subject to state or local 
taxation).
Increases in principal are taxable for the year in which they occur, even if the TIPS 
havenâ€™t matured (so the investor hasnâ€™t actually received a payment of principal).
Deflation adjustments in TIPS decrease the taxpayerâ€™s cost basis.
TIPS can be used to estimate the expected inflation rate.33
34

i= inflation rate

     or  equivalently,    r i i rï² ï²ï‚» ï€­ ï‚» ï€­
36Why TIPS yields are like real yields (to a first approximation):
ï‚·If normal government bonds have a nominal yield of 4%, and TIPS for the same maturity have a yield of 2.5%, the 
marketâ€™s equilibrium expectation is that the annualized average CPI rate will be 1.5% (i.e., that the TIPS inflation 
adjustments will provide an additional 1.5% return), Why? 
â€• The r = 4% market yield on normal government bonds is a nominal yield since it is based on the scheduled 
coupon and principal payments which are nominal CFs because they wonâ€™tbe adjusted for inflation.  
â€• The ï²= 2.5% market yield on TIPs is a real yield since it is based on the scheduled coupon and principal 
payments which are real CFs because they willbe adjusted for inflation.  
â€• Hence, the annualized average inflation rate iexpected by the market must be i ï‚»r -ï²= 4% -2.5% = 1.5%.   
ï‚·If actual inflation rate iexceeds 1.5% annually, the TIPS bondholders will receive a higher real return (of ï²= 2.5%) 
than the nominal bondholders (of ï²= 4% -i< 2.5%). This 1.5% threshold is referred to as the â€œinflation break-
even pointâ€ at which TIPS will have the same value as normal government bonds.
ï‚·Since we know that the real yield equals the nominal yield minus inflation, this also implies that the 2.5% = 4% -
1.5% yield on the TIPS represents the marketâ€™s expectation of the real yield.
ï‚·Why are TIPS yields like real yields only â€œto a first approximationâ€?  
â€• Because there is (1) an illiquidity premium and (2) an inflation-protection premium, i.e., investors are willing 
to sacrifice some yield in order to receive protection against inflation.  So 2.5% is actually a little  < 4% -i.35

19U.S. Treasury Floating Rate Notes (FRNs)
ï‚·U.S. Treasury began issuing in January 2014

oDetermined â€œin arrearsâ€ for each individual coupon date

U.S. Treasury Floating Rate Notes (FRNs) continued
ï‚·Coupon rate = 13-week T-bill auction rate + FRN spread
oT-bills are auctioned every week
oFRN spread is determined on FRN issue date
oFixed FRN spread doesnâ€™t change over the entire FRN term
oThis means that actual FRN coupons have both floating and fixed components
oActual FRNs arenâ€™t purely floating like the idealized FRNs in Chapter 3
oFRNs should be valued as a combination of a floating rate note and a fixed 
rate note

22Municipal Bonds (Munis), continued
ï‚·Chapter 4 focuses on AAA munis and implied tax rates (versus default risk)
ï‚·U.S. government bond coupons taxable at federal level, tax-free at state and local level
ï‚·Municipal bond coupons tax-free at the federal, state and local levels (within same state)
oHence munis are often called â€œtriple tax freeâ€
ï‚·These are the general tax rules, but there can be complications: 
oCapital gains from municipal bond sales are taxable at federal and state levels
oMunicipal bond interest can be subject to federal Alternative Minimum Tax (
AMT) for â€œprivate activityâ€ bonds
ï‚·KEY POINT:  In general, if government bonds are held to maturity, 
oMunicipal govâ€™t bond coupons are tax-free 
oFederal govâ€™t bond coupons are taxed at federal level
43
Since muni coupons are federally tax-free whereas U.S. Treasury coupons are federally 
taxed, the bonds must be priced so that 
â€¢after-tax Treasury yields are approximately equal to tax-free muni yields 
â€¢i.e., munis donâ€™t have to yield as much as treasuries because muni coupons are tax-free.  
More precisely, taking into account the possibility of muni default and risk premium,
Municipal yield = (1- ï´) Treasury yield + Expected loss rate + Additional risk premium
= (1-ï´) Treasury yield + ï°ï¬+ ï«
where as in Chapter 3,
ï´= (federal) tax rate 
ï°= annual default rate of the bond
ï¬= loss rate = % of value lost upon default
ï«= risk premium (as a percentage)
4443

ï´= (Treasury yield -Municipal yield  + ï°ï¬+ ï«)/Treasury yield

â€¢Risk-free rate (1.5 years) = 1.39% 
â€¢Total credit spread = 2.42% -1.39% = 1.03%
â€¢Probability of default = 0.22% (Moodyâ€™s Baa 1 year)
â€¢LGD = 60% (assumed)
â€¢Expected loss = 0.0022 x 0.60 = 0.13%
â€¢Additional risk premium = 1.03% -0.13% = 0.90%53

Valuing a Defaultable Bond â€“Exact Formula
Key points:
ï‚·LGD is measured relative to book value, not market value; why? 
oThere may not be any reported market value with loans/private placements.
ï‚·Book value = PV of scheduled bond payments at scheduled contractual rate
ï‚·Value of defaultable bond = PV of default-adjusted CFs at risk-free rate
ï‚·Estimated PDs correspond to letter ratings 
ï‚·LGDs determined based on country, industry, seniority, loan security, other factors.
ï‚·Moodyâ€™s overall average LGD for all U.S. loans in database = 60%.
5453

3/11/2025
28Example:
ï‚·Bond has scheduled annual coupons of $55 
ï‚·Scheduled principal repayment is $1000 in 5 years. 
ï‚·Simple annual risk-free rate r = 4%, 
ï‚·Annual probability of default (PD) ï°=3%
ï‚·Loss given default (LGD) ï¬= 60%. 
ï‚·What is the expected value of the bond? 
The text lists four steps to the exact formula valuation process: 
1. Compute the probability distribution associated with the default dates 
2. For each default date, determine the cash flows conditional on default 
3. For each default date, find the PV of default-adjusted cash flows at the risk-free rate 
4. Value the cash flows by computing the expected value over all the possible default dates

ï°= probability of default in each year

etc., etc.

4/8/2025
11Valuation for Financial Engineering  --textbook by David C. Shimko
Chapter 5:  Simulation
Methods of Valuing Assets (and Liabilities):
1. Closed-form solutions (as studied in Chapters 1-4)
2. Binomial and trinomial trees
3. Numerical integration
4. Numerical solution of partial differential equations
5. Simulation
Valuation methods 1-4 restrict model complexity.
Simulation is the slowest and least accurate, but also the most flexible if many random 
variables, e.g.:
ï‚·Interest rates
ï‚·Exchange rates
ï‚·Commodity prices
ï‚·Event risks
2What is the simulation process, in a nutshell?  
ï‚·Generate a large # of random outcomes of your model;
ï‚·Calculate the average outcome (i.e., the sample mean);
ï‚·Find some statistical measure of reliability of the result.
Contrast this with Chapters 1-4, in which we
ï‚·Valued deterministic models via the GVE or brute force;
ï‚·Valued simplifiedstochastic models by calculating:
oExpected Present Values (EPVs) of CFs , or equivalently
oPresent Values of Expected (PVEs) CFs
using closed-form (i.e., â€œanalyticâ€) methods
ï‚·(â€œdeterministicâ€ means non-random; â€œstochasticâ€ means random)1
2

4/8/2025
2Todayâ€™s toolkit
â€¢Simulating any random variable
â€¢Estimating the mean of a function of random variables
â€¢Error ranges
â€¢Variance reduction
â€¢Simulating correlated random variables
â€¢Nonlinear â€œcorrelationsâ€
â€¢Optimization
3
Â© 2020 David C. Shimko. All rights reserved.

distributed random numbers3

47Observations:  
ï‚·Let Z ~N(0,1) be a standard normal random variable with CDF N(z) = P(Z â‰¤ z).  
ï‚·Since probabilities are always numbers between 0 and 1, the range of N(z) =[0, 1].
ï‚·The random variable Z can take any value z Îµ[-âˆ, âˆ], i.e, any real number
ï‚·Hence N(z) maps the real line [-âˆ, âˆ] ï‚®[0, 1]
ï‚·Let U ~U[0, 1] be a uniformly distributed random variable over [0, 1]
ï‚·Hence U can take any value u Îµ[0, 1]
ï‚·Suppose N(z) = u where u is a value of U in [0, 1]
ï‚·Let N-1be the inverse function of the CDF N(z).
ï‚·Then z = N-1(u), i.e., the inverse of the CDF of Z maps the value of U to the value of Z.
ï‚·This suggests that Z is equivalent to the random variable defined by N-1(U).
8ï‚·This works with any distribution, not just the normal distribution.
oSuppose X is any random variablewith CDF F(x), and U ~U[0, 1]
oP(F-1(U) â‰¤x) = P(U â‰¤F(x))     (since any CDF F(x) must be an increasing function)
oP(U â‰¤F(x)) = F(x)                    (since the CDF of U is U(x) = x on [0, 1])
oThis ïƒF-1(U) has CDF F(x)
oThis ïƒF-1(U) and X are equivalent         (since they have the same distribution)
ï‚·So, to generate random numbers from any distribution 
oFirst generate U[0, 1] pseudorandom numbers:   u
oThen calculate the inverse CDF of those numbers:   F-1(u)7
8

4/8/2025
59ï‚·In Excel, uniformly distributed random numbers can be generated with u = RAND()
ï‚·In somecases, a formula for the inverse CDF F-1(u) of the desired distribution can be derived
ï‚·The single line F-1(RAND()) will then generate a random number from the desired distribution.
ï‚·In othercases a formula for the inverse CDF F-1(u) canâ€™tbe derived, e.g., the normal distribution 
ï‚·However, for the normal distribution, this can easily be addressed in Excel or Python
ï‚·In Excel the inverse standard normal CDF is given by N-1(u) = NORMSINV(u)
ï‚·The single line = NORMSINV(RAND()) will generate a standard normal distributed random number 
ï‚·When the formula is copied, each cell provides a new random number 
ï‚·A similar function is available in Python: numpy.random.normal(mean, stdev, size).

12THE SIMULATION CORE LOGIC
ï‚·Suppose we want to simulate a large number of values of z of a random 
variable Z
oFor example, you can think of the random variable Z as being the 
time until default of a defaultable bond from Chapter 4
ï‚·We want to know the expected value of f(Z), a function of the random 
variable Z 
oFor example, you can think of the function f(Z) as being the present 
value of the cash flows as a function of the time until default; then 
f(Z) = PV(Z), and we want to know E[PV(Z)].  
oIn chapter 4, we were able to find an analytic closed form solution in 
the simple case
ï‚·We also want to define our maximum likely error based on a 2 standard 
deviation range (though we might like to use 3 or more standard 
deviations for critical applications)11

Every simulation does it like this
â€¢Generate a large number N of random numbers or vectors z
â€¢Compute outcome values f(z)
â€¢Compute the average value ğ‘‹à´¤of the f(z) results ---this is your estimate of E[f(z)]
â€¢Compute the standard deviation of the values of f(z).  Letâ€™s call this â€œsâ€
â€¢Compute the standard error , which is the standard deviation divided by ğ‘, i.e. 
ğ‘ /ğ‘
â€¢Compute the maximum likely error, which is m times the standard error  = 
ğ‘šğ‘ /ğ‘(m is a matter of choice corresponding to the number of standard 
errors)
â€¢If the maximum likely error is too large, increase the size of N in proportion to 
the square of the desired increase in precision.
â€¢The solution is then given by ğ¸ğ‘“ğ‘§=ğ‘‹à´¤Â±ğ‘šğ‘ /ğ‘

1223ï‚·Begin the simulation by generating 1000 values of z in Excel, by copying the

4/8/2025
1325ï‚·Our estimate of the value of E[f(Z)] = E[max(Z,0.5)] from the simulation is 0.6974 +/-
0.0250. 
ï‚·The true theoretical value of Âµ = 0.69779 falls within that range. 
ï‚·If we needed to reduce the size of the maximum likely error (.0250) by a factor of 30, 
we could multiply the number of simulations by 302=  900 for a total of 1,000 x 900 = 
900,000 simulations. 
ï‚·Note that the randomized z values using 1,000 simulations have a mean of 0.03 and a 
standard deviation of 0.98, not the true theoretical values of 0 and 1. 
ï‚·Of course, if we are simulating any set of random numbers, we will get values of the 
mean and standard deviations changing with every simulation, i.e. every recalculation 
of the spreadsheet. 
ï‚·We can use variance reduction techniques to improve on the simulated values of z to 
get more accurate simulation results.

1427Moment Matching
ï‚·One simple and effective way to reduce sampling variance is to force the first two 
moments, i.e. the mean and standard deviation of the random sample of z values 
to be equal to their theoretical values, i.e. 0 and 1. 
ï‚·This can be accomplished by replacing each simulated value of z with its 
standardized equivalent, that is, 
z zzsï€­ï‚¬
where we subtract the sample mean from each value of z, and divide 
by the sample standard deviation. 
ï‚·This technique, called moment matching , eliminates two sources of 
uncertainty from the random sample and can have a positive impact on 
simulation performance.

4/8/2025
1631Antithetic Sampling
ï‚·Each time we choose a uniformly distributed random number uifor i= 1, â€¦ M, we 
include the value of (1 â€“ui) for i= 1, â€¦ M as a separate random number, for a total of 
N = 2M random numbers. 
ï‚·For the normal distribution, this has the effect of forcing the mean of the N = 2M 
sample random numbers {zi= F-1 (ui),  -zi=  F-1 (1 â€“ui), i= 1, â€¦ M} to be zero
ï‚·But this does not affect the variance, since ziand â€“ziare included in every simulation. 
ï‚·However, this does create negative correlations between observations in the dataset, 
allowing for some variance reduction. 
ï‚·Antithetic sampling used to be considered beneficial because the computation time 
for generating an antithetic number (1-ui) was less than the time of creating a new 
pseudorandom number. 
ï‚·This benefit is not as highly valued now due to advances in computing speed and 
parallel processing.
ï‚·P.S.  The adjective â€œantitheticâ€ (= â€œantitheticalâ€) refers to things that contradict, 
oppose, clash, or cancel each other out.  â€œAnthesisâ€ is the related noun.
32Unpacking these statements further:
ï‚·We know that if Z has CDF F(z) and U ~U[0, 1]
oP(F-1(U) ï‚£z) = P(U ï‚£F(z)) = F(z)
oHence the CDF of F-1(U) is F(z), which ïƒF-1(U) ~ Z
ï‚·But if U ~U[0, 1] and x Îµ (0, 1),
oP(1 â€“U ï‚£x) = P(U ï‚³1 â€“x) = 1 â€“P(U ï‚£1 â€“x) = 1 â€“(1 â€“x) = x
oThis implies the CDF of 1 â€“U is G(x) = x, which ïƒ1 â€“U ~U[0, 1]
ï‚·Hence if Z has CDF F(z) and U ~U[0, 1],
oNot onlyis it true that F-1(U) ~ Z; 
obut it is alsotrue that F-1(1 â€“U) ~ Z
othis is the basis for antithetic sampling31
32

4/8/2025
1733ï‚·Suppose zi= F-1(ui) and wi= F-1(1 â€“ui)
ï‚·Then F(zi) = uiand F(wi) = 1 â€“ui
ï‚·F(zi) = P(Z ï‚£zi) = 1 â€“P(Z ï‚³zi)
ï‚·If the pdf of Z is symmetric around z = 0 (as it is for Z ~ N(0,1))
oP(Z ï‚³zi) = P(Zï‚£â€“zi) = F(â€“zi)
ï‚·Hence F(zi) = 1 â€“F(â€“zi), which ïƒF(â€“zi) = 1 â€“F(zi) = 1 -ui
ï‚·But F(wi) = 1 â€“ui, so if F is strictly increasing (as it is for Z ~ N(0,1)),wi= â€“zi
ï‚·Hence it is true that ziand â€“ziare included in every simulation
ï‚·This is the theory.  But in practice, once you calculate the zi= F-1(ui) values, 
you can simply negate them directly.
ï‚·In other words, you do not need to calculate â€“zi= F-1(1 â€“ui) from scratch; 
that is, you do not need to calculate them indirectly from the 1 â€“uivalues

1937Example
ï‚·Recall the setup for our previous simulation of 1000 values:
oWe generated 1000 values of z from N(0,1) in Excel, by copying the formula 
â€œ=normsinv(rand()) â€ into 1000 cells in a column. 
oThen for each cell, we computed the value of f(z) = max(z,0.50). 
oFinally, we computed the sample mean (as our estimate of E[f(Z)] = E[max(Z,0.5]), 
sample standard deviation, and standard error of the f(z) values. 
ï‚·To test the impact of moment matching and antithetic sampling, we will now perform 
1000 iterations of our simulation of 1000 values, for a total of 1,000,000 values. 
ï‚·To judge the efficiency of the variance reduction, we will compute the sample standard 
deviation of the results of every 1000 sims and then compile the results in a two stage 
process.
ï‚·In Excel, therefore, we will use our original setup to simulate 1000 outcomes, and then 
use a what-if table to generate 1000 results of the 1000 individual simulations. The 
results are summarized in the table below.
38
37
38

4/8/2025
2039ï‚·The first three columns contain the raw z-values, the z values with 
corrected mean (0) and standard deviation (1), and the antithetic 
values; entries 501-1000 are the negative values of 1-500  (we are 
only showing the first 10 simulations). 
ï‚·The next 3 columns show the values of f(z) for the three methods. 
ï‚·The final table on the right tabulates 1000 simulations of the results 
of the single simulation done in the spreadsheet (we are only 
showing the first 10 of the 1000 simulations of the rsultsof the 
single simulation). 
ï‚·For each column, the statistics are computed on the top. 
40ï‚·Using the basic technique, or the z column, the standard deviations of the 
1000-sim values was 0.0127, which as expected, were lower by a factor of 
about sqrt(1000) from the original f(z) simulation result of 0.3956. 
ï‚·Remarkably, the moment matching technique reduced the standard error 
by a factor of more than 3.5 times, from 0.0004 to 0.0001. 
ï‚·The antithetic technique did not have much success with this particular 
problem, though in other problems it may be much more successful. 
ï‚·In all cases, the large number of final simulations leads to good results for 
all three techniques. 
ï‚·Perhaps needless to say, different variance reduction techniques work 
better with different simulations, but the results can be significant.39

2141Simulation and the Defaultable Bond Value
Recall the simulation core logic:
ï‚·Suppose we want to simulate a large number of values of x of a random variable X
oFor example, you can think of the random variable X as being the time until 
default of a defaultable bond from Chapter 4
ï‚·We want to know the expected value of f(X), a function of the random variable X 
oFor example, you can think of the function f(X) as being the present value of 
the cash flows as a function of the time until default; then f(X) = PV(X), and we 
want to know E[PV(X)].  
oIn chapter 4, we were able to find an analytic closed form solution in the 
simple case
ï‚·We also want to define our maximum likely error based on a 2 or 3 standard 
deviation range

Chapter 4.

3059Multivariate Simulations
ï‚·Until now we have only been simulating one random variable at a time.
ï‚·In practice we need to simulate many random variables simultaneously. 
ï‚·If the random variables were independent, we could simulate them separately.
ï‚·But in most situations the random variables arenâ€™t independent. 
oFor example, think of the bonds in a bond portfolio
ï‚·We usually expect random variables to have specified correlations. 
ï‚·Pearson correlation is the measure of the strength of a linear relationship applied to 
jointly normally distributed random variables.  
oIt makes sense to start with this. 
ï‚·First, letâ€™s simulate two N(0,1) random variables Z1and Z2with a given correlation ï². 
oBut how can we do this?
60ï‚·Try starting with two independent N(0,1) random variables Z1and Z3(which we can 
simulate separately). 
ï‚·We can keep Z1and then compute Z2as a linear combination of Z1and Z3.
ï‚·In other words, set Z2= aZ1+ bZ3and try to solve for coefficients a and b such that:
oThe mean of Z2must be 0
oThe variance of Z2must be 1 
oThe correlation between Z1and Z2must be ï²
ï‚·The first condition is automatically true:
oE[Z2] = E[aZ1+ bZ3} = aE[Z1] + bE[Z3] = 0 + 0 = 0
oUnfortunately this gives us noinformation about a and b!59
60

4/8/2025
3161ï‚·The second condition implies: 
oVar(Z2) = Var(aZ1+ bZ3) = a2Var(Z1) + b2Var(Z3) = a2+ b2= 1
oHere we are using that Z1and Z3are independent N(0,1)
ï‚·Finally, the third condition implies:

80ï‚·When simulating more than two correlated normal random numbers, the 
Cholesky decomposition is invaluable. 
ï‚·In this case, letâ€™s suppose we have k random variables with correlation 
matrix A, and we want to simulate N sets of random variables. 
ï‚·We first produce an Nxkmatrix of uncorrelated random z values. 
ï‚·We then post-multiply the data by the transpose of the Cholesky 
decomposition of A. The resulting matrix of random numbers has the 
desired correlation matrix.
ï‚·The appendix of Chapter 5 covers the construction of a Cholesky 
decomposition, moment matching to â€œpurifyâ€ the sample data, and the 
Cholesky transformation to create data with the specified covariance matrix.
ï‚·In the following slides, weâ€™ll walk through these ideas in more detail.79
80

4/8/2025
4181Computing the Cholesky Decomposition of a Covariance Matrix
â€¢In Chapter 5, we derived a 2 x 2 upper triangular matrix such that we could post-multiply 
two independent (hence uncorrelated) N(0,1) random variables Z1and Z3by it to produce 
two correlated N(0,1) random variables Z1and Z2with a given correlationmatrix.    
â€¢We verified that if we pre-multiplied this upper triangular matrix by its lower triangular 
transpose, we reproduced the 2 x 2 correlation matrix of  Z1and Z2.  We called the lower 
triangular matrix the â€œlower triangular Cholesky decompositionâ€ of the correlation matrix.
â€¢We then showed that in the context of nonstandardnormal random variables, there was a 
similar 2 x 2 upper triangular matrix  such that we could post-multiply two independent 
(hence uncorrelated) N(0,1) random variables Z1and Z3by it and add the means to 
produce two correlated nonstandardnormal random variables X1and X2with the given 
means and given covariancematrix.   
â€¢We also verified that if we pre-multiplied this upper triangular matrix by its lower 
triangular transpose, we reproduced the 2 x 2 covariancematrix of X1and X2.  
82We now consider how to determine the lower triangular Cholesky decomposition 
of any given covariance matrix A.  Denoting this lower triangular matrix by R, and 
its upper triangular transpose by RT, we seek to find R such that RRT= A.  
Letâ€™s first consider the 2 x 2 case.  Since R is lower triangular, the equation RRT= A 
implies that:

the second column of A with the second column of R, and so on.81

4/8/2025
4997Additional Notes on Cholesky Decomposition
We now know 
ï‚·how a Cholesky decomposition of a covariance matrix A of any 
dimension can be computed, and
ï‚·how to use the decomposition to simulate any number of 
correlated normal random variables
ï‚·how to specialize this to simulation of standardnormal random 
variables using the Cholesky decomposition of their correlation
matrix.
98However, we need to be a little more careful.  
ï‚§The procedure we developed for finding the Cholesky decomposition of a 
covariance matrix A provides an algorithm for solving for the entries rijof the 
matrix R in terms of the entries aijof the covariance matrix A  such that RRT= A. 
ï‚§But so far we have always tacitly assumed that the equations for the entries rij
in terms of aijhad a real(as opposed to non-real or complex) solution.  
ï‚§It turns out that the decomposition algorithm equations will have real solutions 
if (and only if) the matrix A is positive definite .  
ï‚§We wonâ€™t provide a proof in this course, but proofs are readily available, and 
can be based on induction from the 2 x 2 case that we earlier solved 
completely from first principles.97
98

4/8/2025

4/8/2025
51101ï‚·The truecovariance matrix A of the k random variables will always be positive definite, 
and hence the Cholesky decomposition algorithm will produce the desired factorization.
ï‚·However, in practice, the truecovariance matrix A of the k random variables may not be 
known, and will instead be estimated based on sample data.  
ï‚·Depending on how the sample data has been compiled, the estimated covariance 
matrix A may not be positive definite, and the Cholesky decomposition algorithm and 
attempt at simulation may fail.  
ï‚·More information on these issues is of course available in the literature.
102Copulaeand the Correlation of Non-Normal Random Variables 
ï‚·We saw how to convert simulated normal independent random numbers into correlated 
random numbers. 
ï‚·However, this approach only works with normally distributed random variables, so how 
can we deal with non-normal random variables?
ï‚·Suppose we have two bonds. 
oBond A       default rate = ï°A= .05     default date = XA(geometrically distributed r.v.)
oBond B       default rate = ï°B= .10    default date = XB(geometrically distributed.r.v.)
ï‚·We would like to introduce systemic risk to the analysis of our bond portfolio, so we will 
assume an 80% correlation in the default dates. 
oï²(XA,XB) = .80  (Note:  For 3 or more bonds, assume a specified correlation matrix.)
ï‚·We can generate random numbers as values of normally distributed random variables Z1
and Z2with a correlation of 80% using the Cholesky method 
oï²( Z1, Z2) = .80101
102

4/8/2025
52103To convert these to geometrically distributed default dates, we may first convert them to uniform 
numbers by computing the normal CDF, and then convert them to geometric distributions using the 
inverse CDF of the geometric distribution. 
oWe know the normal CDF N(t) = P(Z ï‚£t) maps (-âˆ, âˆ) to (0, 1)
oObserve that P(N(Z) ï‚£t) = P(Z ï‚£N-1(t)) = N(N-1(t)) = t  
oThis implies that the random variable N(Z) ~U[0,1] is uniformly distributed over [0, 1]
oThus weâ€™ve converted random numbers z1and z2into uniform numbers u1= N(z1) and u2= N(z2)
oThen let H-1(u1) = log(1 â€“u1)/log(1 -.05) and G-1(u2) = log(1 â€“u2)/log(1 -.10) be inverse CDFs of the 
geometric distributions with parameters ï°A= .05 and ï°B= .10    
oBut donâ€™t forget to round to integer values as we did previously for the single bond default dates:
 log(1 ) log(1 )1      or alternatively     ,0log(1 ) log(1 )u ui INT i ROUNDUPï° ï°ï€­ ï€­ïƒ¦ ïƒ¶ ïƒ¦ ïƒ¶ï€½ ï€« ï€½ïƒ§ ïƒ· ïƒ§ ïƒ·ï€­ ï€­ïƒ¨ ïƒ¸ ïƒ¨ ïƒ¸
oThen H-1(u1) = H-1(N(z1)) and G-1(u2) = G-1(N(z2)) are geometrically distributed random numbers
oThis is an example of a Gaussian copula, the calculations for which are shown in the text:

53105ï‚·The first two columns are random z values with a correlation of 0.80. 
ï‚·The second two columns are the CDFs or uniform equivalents N(z) of those values. 
ï‚·The third set of two columns are the transformed default date simulations using the 
inverse functions H-1and G-1of the geometric CDF.
ï‚·The graphs illustrate the impact of correlation in defaults on the timing of joint default. 
ï‚·As expected, when defaults are positively correlated, we see many more outcomes 
where both defaults occur within 10 years of the starting date, as compared to the 
simulations with the negative correlation. 
ï‚·The copula can be used to capture the essence of correlation while remaining true to 
the geometric distribution model of bond default dates.

106Simulation and Optimization 
ï‚·We want to optimize some function of one (or more) random variables. 
ï‚·The text considers a simplified inventory management problem. 
ï‚·Let â€œsâ€ = # units of a product purchased and stocked in inventory
ï‚·Let â€œDâ€ = # units demanded by customers; 
ï‚·Assume D is a geometric random variable with P(D = d) = (1 - ï°)d-1ï°with ï°= .10
ï‚·Expected demand is therefore E[D] = 1/ ï°= 1/.10 = 10 units. 
ï‚·# units sold = min (s, D). 
ï‚·Assume gross profit for each unit sold = $5. 
ï‚·Assume overstocking leads to return or storage costs of $3 per unit
ï‚·Overstocked quantity = max (s -D, 0) ïƒcost of overstocking = $3 x max (s-D, 0) 
ï‚·Assume understocking leads to disappointed customers costing $7 per unit
ï‚·Understocked quantity = max (D -s,0) ïƒcost of understocking = $7 x max (D â€“s, 0)

54107ï‚·We want to stock the correct # units s to maximize E [net profit (s, D). 
ï‚·One might guess s = E (D) = 10, but since the cost of overstocking is lower than the cost 
of understocking, it would be better to err on the side of having s > 10. But how much 
greater? 
ï‚·With the geometric distribution, the probability of higher demand levels is decreasing. 
ï‚·In addition, the choice variable s is integer-valued, so calculus wonâ€™t help. 
ï‚·Therefore, we use simulation to estimate E[net profit(s, D)] for each value of s, and then 
choose the value of s that maximizes E[net profit(s, D)]. 
ï‚§Letting F(d) = P(D ï‚£d) = 1 â€“(1 - ï°)d, simulate random numbers d = #units demanded via:

108ï‚·Then for each simulated value of d, calculate simulated net profit (s, d) and the

Â© 2020 David C. Shimko. All rights reserved.ï‚·However, because of the natural errors in the simulation process, the result 
might appear like the choppy curve in the diagram below from the text: 107
108

4/8/2025
55109ï‚·Fortunately, another variance reduction method can be employed. 
ï‚·If we fix the random numbers for our experiment, and then calculate expected 
profit for each given level of s, we obtain the smooth line is the chart, which is 
easily seen to be optimized at s = 12. 
ï‚·This is known as the method of common random numbers: In comparisons when 
an inference will be drawn about the change in the simulation result, use the 
same set of random numbers for each simulation.
ï‚·One weakness of this approach:  It uses fewer random numbers than the fully 
random method
ï‚·Therefore, any analysis you do of this type should be checked with multiple sets 
of fixed random numbers, each of which will provide a specific optimal solution. 
ï‚·For example, if we had a â€œbadâ€ set of random numbers in our optimization 
above, we may have arrived at the incorrect optimal value, having biased all the 
profitability estimates in the same way.
Conclusions
â€¢Any random variable can be simulated by the function F-1(u), where F-1is the inverse CDF of u, 
and u is a uniformly distributed random variable
â€¢To simulate the value of a function of number of uncorrelated random variables (any 
distributions), follow these steps
â€¢Generate N values and calculate the mean
â€¢Calculate the standard deviation, and divide by ğ‘to get the standard error
â€¢Report the mean as your estimate and your â€œmaximum errorâ€ as 2 times the standard 
error
â€¢Increase the sample size if more precision is needed
â€¢To correlate normally distributed random variables, use independent random simulations and 
multiply the data vector by the transpose of the Choleskydecomposition of the desired 
covariance matrix
â€¢Correlation is only defined for normal random variables
â€¢To correlate random variables of any distribution, convert correlated normal random numbers 
to uniform distributions, and then use F-1(u) to produce the desired random variables

Chapter 6: Stochastic Processes in Finance
Chapter 5 â€“simulation of one or more RVs at a point in time
Chapter 6 â€“simulation of stochastic processes , such as:
ï‚·Future financial asset prices
ï‚·Future interest rates
ï‚·Future commodity prices
ï‚·Future cash flows
In this chapter weâ€™ll also learn how to:
ï‚·Use historical data to estimate the parameters of these stochastic processes
ï‚·Calibrate these processes to assumed future probability distributions
The Random Walk
ï‚·Random walks were used by French mathematician Louis Bachelierto describe stock 
price movements on the Paris Bourse (i.e., the Paris stock exchange).
ï‚·Random walks are one of the simplest of all stochastic processes
Efficient Market Hypothesis:
ï‚·We may not believe that price changes are random.
ï‚·However, we generally have the same or lessinformation than the market does
ï‚·Therefore, we canâ€™t expect to forecast prices better than the market 
ï‚·Price changes might as well be modeled as random, even if theyâ€™re not.
But our goals go beyond price forecasting:
ï‚·To capture potential volatility of future prices
ï‚·To capture potential correlations between future prices

10Financial Applications of the ARW
Bachelierused the ARW model for stock price fluctuations in the short term.
Problem:           may become negative o ver longer time periodst jSï€«
                          However, price s of stocks with limited liability can't  ever go negative
Some financial variables cango negative, such as cash flows (CFs) of a company
ï‚·If CFs  << 0, company may go bankrupt or go out of business 
ï‚·Nevertheless, a cash flow model could be reasonably modeled using the ARW.
19
Another possible application for the ARW is a â€œbasisâ€:
ï‚·Basis = difference between two prices.  For example:
ï‚·S = West Texas (WTI) oil price minus North Sea (Brent) oil price
ï‚·The difference canbecome negative, like CFs.
ï‚·This fact seems to support using the ARW to model a basis.
On the other hand, as  j , the standard d eviation of an ARW process ;  
i.e., the standard deviation of  increases indefinitely as the square root of ti me.t jj
Sï³
ï€«ï‚­ï‚¥ ï€½ ï‚­ï‚¥
But we wouldn't expect the difference be tween two prices to increase inefinitely .
Instead, we'd expect there to be a relat ionship between the two prices drawing 
them back together over time. 
We'd like to construct a model that can reflect such long-term joint relationshi ps
between two RVs.
To achieve this we now consider a differ ent process that is mean-reverting.
2019

2345Additional comments on OU process:
Recall that
2
lim [ ]    and    limVar[ ](2 )t j t jj jE S Sï³ï­ï« ï«ï€« ï€«ï‚®ï‚¥ ï‚®ï‚¥ï€½ ï€½ï€­
and neither of these limits depends on the starting value St.
This means that the long term mean and 2-standard deviation
confidence interval around future values of St+jdonâ€™t depend
upon the specific trajectory/path; see the text diagram.
46In view of its convergence to the mean, the OU process is
a natural choice for modeling the WTI â€“Brent oil basis mentioned
earlier, or for modeling interest rates (assuming that there will be
reversion to a mean).
The OU process is not suitable for modeling stock prices or stock indices,
because that would imply there is a predictable long term mean value
irrespective of the current trajectory/path, which doesnâ€™t match market
expectations (our best source for what is â€œtrueâ€).  
Therefore, for stock prices or stock indices, a different model is needed.45

2651Geometric Random Walk (GRW)
We have seen that neither the ARW process nor the OU process is suitable for 
modeling stock prices or stock indices over the long term.  
ï‚·For both processes, the forecast variables St+jare normally distributed, which 
means there is a non-zero probability that St+jwill be a negative value, 
whereas limited liability means that this will never happen.  
ï‚·In addition, the OU process implies that there is a predictable long term 
mean price, irrespective of the current price, and this isnâ€™t consistent with 
market expectations.
ï‚·Sothe ARW process has one strike against it, and 
ï‚·the OU process, although it has some attractive features, has two strikes 
against it.  
Letâ€™s go back to the ARW process (â€œone of the simplest of all stochastic 
processesâ€), andtry to see where we went wrong.
52With the benefit of hindsight, we can spot the problem in the very first step, i.e., 
the defining equation:
