70In summary,
The best model for security prices is the Geometric Brownian Motion (GBM), which is 
the GRW with an infinitesimal time step dt.  Advantages of this model include:
ï‚·It ensures prices can never become negative (limited liability);
ï‚·A constant proportional dividend rate can be subtracted from the drift to obtain post-
dividend simulations
Disadvantages of this model include:
ï‚·It assumes constant expected returns and volatility;
ï‚·It doesnâ€™t allow security values to reach zero in the event of bankruptcy or termination
ï‚·It does not allow negatively skewed or leptokurtic returns in the simple version
(Negatively skewed means sudden drops in price are more likely than sudden rises.  A 
leptokurtic distribution has kurtosis greater than the normal distribution = 3; leptokurtic 
returns have probability of extreme returns greater than for a normal distribution, i.e., the 
distribution has fat tails.)
69
70

4/15/2025
3671Best model for commodity prices and interest rates is the mean reverting process.
ï‚·Commodities arenâ€™t assets in the financial sense.  
ï‚·High commodity prices a) stimulate production and b) reduce demand leading to 
expectation of lower prices in the future.
ï‚·If you try to short commodities (i.e., sell now at a higher price for delivery in the 
future at a lower price) you would find no market for short sales
ï‚·Low commodity prices a) reduce production and b) stimulate demand leading to 
expectation of higher prices in the future.
ï‚·Interest rates resemble commodities, since high rate encourage lending and 
discourage borrowing, i.e., stimulate production and reduce demand, leading to 
expectation of lower rates in the future.
ï‚·Finally, in many financial models volatility is assumed to change over time, and this 
happens in a mean reverting process.
72The ABM process which is the ARW with an infinitesimal time step dt, can be used 
to model most financial variables over the short term, but generally fails long-term 
due to
ï‚·The possibility of negative values, and
ï‚·Increasing volatility over time.
So the main function of the ABM/ARW process at this point is to provide a 
foundation for the OU mean reverting process and the GMB/GRW process.
71
72

1Valuation for Financial Engineering  --textbook by David C. Shimko
Chapter 7: Risk, Capital and Valuation
In Chapter 7, we will study 
ï‚·how risk is measured, 
ï‚·how risk affects required returns, 
ï‚·how risk relates to capital, and 
ï‚·how risk affects asset valuations.
Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko4/16/2024 1
What is meant by â€œriskâ€?
ï‚·In previous chapters we valued life contingent insurance benefits and defaultable 
corporate bonds.  To determine their risk-adjusted values, we discounted the 
expected or promised cashflows at the risk-free interest rate plus an adjustment
such as Ï€, Î´, Îº+Ï€Î» orÎ¸+Ï€Î», as appropriate depending on the circumstances.
ï‚·When cash flows are contingent, the discount rate (required return) is frequently 
higherthan the risk-free rate to compensate the investor for the extra risk he/she is 
taking on.
ï‚·However, the discount rate (required return) for contingent cash flows can also be 
lowerthan the risk-free rate.  For example, put options used to insure an 
investment portfolio may be more highly valued (have a lower required return) 
because the put option cash flows are negatively correlated to portfolio cash flows, 
helping to reduce the risk of the portfolio. 
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko21
2

4/19/2025
2What is meant by â€œriskâ€ (continued)?
ï‚·The total dollar risk of a portfolio of positively but not perfectly correlated risks 
increases with each risky asset added, but the risk per dollar invested declines.  Hence 
diversification (holding of a broad-based portfolio with many types of assets) can 
reduce the risk of percentage returns..
ï‚·A financial analyst must choose their definition of risk, and ensure that their risk 
measures reflect the choice.
ï‚·Companies may value risky projects one way, and shareholders may value them 
another way.
ï‚·Financial companies approach the problem differently than nonfinancial companies.
ï‚·Companies may value risk differently from other companies, depending on shareholder 
preferences.
ï‚·This chapter provides tools to value all sorts of risky cash flows in many different 
situations, regardless of how risk is measured and translated into risk charges and 
project valuations.
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko3
Asymmetric Risks
Risks may be assessed differently between two parties entering into a transaction.
Example:  Fixed monthly payment for life received under an annuity contract.
From the annuity purchaserâ€™s perspective, the risks include such things as:
ï‚·Risk of failure of insurance company to make payments if it defaults in the future
ï‚·Risk of reduced payments, delayed payments, or non-payment by state guaranty 
associations 
ï‚·Risk of annuitant dying too soon to collect enough annuity payments to offset 
purchase price (mortality risk)
ï‚·Risk of higher inflation lowering the spending power of fixed annuity payments
ï‚·Risk of living longer than expected without income, if annuity isnâ€™t purchased 
(longevity risk)
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko43
4

4/19/2025
3Asymmetric Risks, continued
But from the insurance companyâ€™s perspective, the risks include such things as:
ï‚·Risk of increased longevity of some or all annuitants, e.g., from medical advances
ï‚·Risk of lower-than-expected interest and inflation rates
ï‚·Risk of higher-than-expected expenses or corporate taxes
ï‚·Risk of inaccurate pricing models, assumptions, or methodologies
ï‚·Unanticipated usage of embedded options
ï‚·Contract holder persistency risk for annual premium annuities
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko5
Insurance can be viewed as an asset for the customer and a liability for the insurer:
ï‚·Customer is willing pay a premium over expected value of the contract in order to 
reduce the risk of living without income
ï‚·Insurance company must charge a premium over the expected value of the 
contract in order to provide for its risk, accumulate surplus and earn a profit

Revisiting â€œReturnâ€
Consider the following examples discussed way back in Chapter 1:
Suppose an investor buys a share of stock for $20 and the price rises to $21.
ï‚·Gain = $21 -$20 = $1
ï‚·% return = $1/$20 = 5%
Now suppose the investor sells short a share of stock for $20 and the price rises to $21.
ï‚·Loss = $21 -$20 = $1
ï‚·% return = -$1/$0 is undefined!  
ï‚·No initial investment means no denominator for the return calculation
We would have the same problem calculating % returns for forwards and futures 
positions
Thus calculating % returns for undercapitalized (or non-capitalized) investments can be 
frustrating.
But calculating % returns can also be frustrating for fully-capitalized investments!
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko1817
18

4/19/2025
10Consider the following example from Dr. Shimkoâ€™stext book:
Letâ€™s suppose you want to invest $100.  It looks like you can expect to make higher 
dollar and % returns by investing in the startup company.
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko19
However, consider this:
ï‚·You can buy $500 worth of the blue-chip stock by investing $100 of your own money and taking a 
loan for the remaining $400.  In the worst-case scenario (because itâ€™s a blue-chip stock), the 
value of the blue-chip stock will decline to 80% of $500, or $400, which will fully repay the loan 
on a risk-free basis.  Moreover, you can expect a return of 10% x $500 = $50, which can be 
viewed as a return of 50% on your capital investment of $100.
ï‚·On the other hand, you can only buy $100 worth of the start-up company stock by investing $100 
of your own money.  The start-up company stock cannot be used as risk-free collateral for 
repaying the loan, because in the worst-case scenario its value may decline to $0.
ï‚·The actual capital required for one share of the blue-chip stock is $20, since the remaining $80 
can be financed on a risk-free basis, whereas the actual capital required for one share of the 
startup company is $100.
ï‚·In general, overcapitalized investments can be divided into one portion that is considered risky 
and another portion that is considered risk-free.
ï‚·This differs sharply from the traditional approach, which attributes the entire return on 
investment to the level of cash deployed.
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko2019
20

4/19/2025
11Example:  Assume a $100 investment in the S&P, with expected return of 12% = $12, 
expected return on T-bills (risk-free rate) = 6%, worst case loss of 25% = $25.
The GVE implies
Time Value of Money + Risk Compensation = Total Expected Return
6% x $100 + Risk Compensation = $12
Risk Compensation = $12 -$6 = $6.
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko21
Under the traditional way of thinking, the capital investment = the entire $100 of cash 
deployed, hence the % return on risk capital would be viewed as being only 
Risk Compensation/Risk Capital = $6/$100 = 6%.
Under the new way of thinking (aka â€œrisk capital formulationâ€ in the text), the risk capital 
= the $25 actually at risk, hence the % return on risk capital would be viewed as being 
Risk Compensation/Risk Capital = $6/$25 = 24%.
In other words, under the traditional way of thinking, the $6 risk compensation is 
attributed to the full $100 investment, whereas under the new way of thinking, it is 
attributed only to the $25 risky portion of the investment.
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko2221
22

4/19/2025
12This section of the text closes with the following remarks:
ï‚·A firm that maximizes return may be doing its shareholders a disservice, because 
return is normalized to the amount of capital employed. 
ï‚·A smaller return on a higher capital base may add significant value to shareholders, 
and therefore, firms should look to value-added rather than return on capital as a 
measure of performance. 
ï‚·The only time it makes sense to maximize return on capital is when capital is the 
limited resource. This is sometimes the case in the real world. 
ï‚·However, under the perfect markets assumptions of traditional finance theory, 
unlimited capital is available from shareholders as long as a competitive return is 
earned. 
ï‚·For this reason, real world practices often differ from recommendations of financial 
theory.
4/16/2024Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --2-24-2020 textbook draft by David C. Shimko23
Risk Capital and RAROC
Risk capital = estimated maximum dollar amount put at risk in an investment
= maximum likely loss
= statistic which may be unrelated to the cash invested
Example:  Short sale of stock at $20, then price rises to $21
Assume the maximum likely loss = $10
Then %return on risk capital = -$1/$10 = -10%
Example: Long position on oil contract, forward price = $100, actual price = $101
Assume the maximum likely loss = $5
The %return on risk capital = $1/$5 = 20%

Sharpe Ratio
The Sharpe Ratio can be viewed as:
ï‚·Cost of risk capital
ï‚·Return on risk capital
ï‚·The most important measure of investment performance
oWhy might one anticipate this to be the case?
oBecause the ratio is measuring return per unit of risk
oWhat could be more important than that????  
The Sharpe ratio can be calculated:
ï‚·On a prospective (i.e., looking forward into the future) basis;
ï‚·On a retrospective (i.e., looking backward into the past) basis.

1Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial

Chapter 8: The Single Period CAPM and CFPM
5/6/2025Introduction
Note how far we have come:
In the firstpart of the course, we only valued assets for which either:
ï‚·We assumed that there was no risk , or
ï‚·We assumed that there was risk, but we discounted expected cash flows (CFs) at 
assumed rates of return
In contrast, nowwe want to figure out appropriate discount rates to value risky CFs.
Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --1-11-2021 textbook draft by David C. Shimko2CAPM stands for â€œCapital Asset Pricing Modelâ€
CAPM denotes the contemporary approach following Sharpe (1964), which 
takes the investorâ€™s point of view and focuses on asset returns given prices, 
rather than asset valuation given cash flows (CFs)
CAPM relies on a framework pioneered by Harry Markowitz, a University of 
Chicago PhD student of the Nobel economist Milton Friedman in 1952, which 
assumes a number of assets with known expected percentage returns and 
standard deviations.  
But we know from the first part of the course that when assets arenâ€™t fully 
funded, percentage returns sometimes canâ€™t be calculated (or if they can be 
calculated, are not very intuitive)!
5/6/20251
2

5/6/2025
2Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --1-11-2021 textbook draft by David C. Shimko3CFPM stands for â€œCash Flow Portfolio Modelâ€
CFPM denotes the Lintner-Mossincash flow based version of CAPM, which 
assumes that all assets produce normally distributed liquidating cash flows in 
one period.  
In this chapter we will cover:
ï‚·Portfolio mathematics
ï‚·How to price risky CFs based on their relationship to the risks of a portfolio
ï‚·How investorsâ€™ risk aversion translates into required RORs/discount rates on CFs
ï‚·Equilibrium expected return and return covariance matrix
ï‚·Variance utility, standard deviation utility, or exponential utility, which all ïƒCAPM 
pricing equations
ï‚·Computation of equilibrium asset prices and portfolio holdings
5/6/2025
Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --1-11-2021 textbook draft by David C. Shimko4Note that CFPM also provides linear subportfolio pricing relationships:
ï‚·Any subportfoliocan be priced relative to any other subportfolio
ï‚·Hence dollar returns on securities can be benchmarked to correlated assets,                       
not just to a single market portfolio of all securities (as typically done under CAPM)

5/6/2025
6Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --1-11-2021 textbook draft by David C. Shimko11 5/6/2025ï‚·Unlimited cash is held or borrowed by each investor as needed to purchase their assets.
ï‚·All assets can be bought or sold in unlimited quantities.
ï‚·All investors have the same information and agree on the cash flow risk parameters
ï‚·All investors have the same utility functions for wealth.
ï‚·Investors differ only in their levels of risk aversion
ï‚·We will ignore taxes and transaction costs
ï‚·Each investor in this economy deploys their initial wealthW0to purchase risky 
assets which, together with any interest earnings or cost will result in their end of 
period wealthW1
Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --1-11-2021 textbook draft by David C. Shimko12 5/6/2025ï‚·If the investor spends lessthan their initial wealth W0, they earn the risk-free 
rate ron the uninvested funds.
ï‚·On the other hand, if the investor spends morethan their initial wealth W0,, 
they pay the risk-free rate ron the borrowed funds.
ï‚·Hence the investorâ€™s end-of-period expected wealth is the expected cash flow 
returned from asset positions plus the impact of the residual wealth invested or 
borrowed at the risk free rate.ï®

Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --1-11-2021 textbook draft by David C. Shimko20 5/6/2025ï‚·Investors maximize expected utility to obtain the asset demand equations, and the 
model applies the market clearing supply condition to determine asset values.
ï‚·In the text, Dr. Shimkosummarizes the results for both the variance and the standard 
deviation utility models, but then subsequently focuses on the variance model.
ï‚·Conclusions for the two models are identical, except for a change in scale of the risk 
aversion parameter.
ï‚·The standard deviation model is included to allow risk to be measured in the same 
units as the cash flows, and to allow the model to be easily calibrated to market-
determined Sharpe ratios.19

Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --1-11-2021 textbook draft by David C. Shimko28 5/6/2025Valuation with Market Clearing (vector of initial security prices/values V):
Recall the following assumptions of our model:
ï‚·Unlimited cash is held or borrowed by each investor as needed to purchase their 
assets.
ï‚·All assets can be bought or sold in unlimited quantities.
ï‚·All investors have the same information and agree on the cash flow risk parameters
ï‚·All investors have the same utility functions for wealth.
ï‚·Investors differ only in their initial levels of wealth and risk aversion
As a result,
ï‚·More risk averse investors take smaller positions in the risky assets, and vice versa; 
ï‚·However, regardless of their wealth, they each invest in the same relative 
proportionsof each of the risky assets27
28

5/6/2025
15Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --1-11-2021 textbook draft by David C. Shimko29 5/6/2025ï‚·They choose the same relative proportions because such proportions provide the 
best risk/reward tradeoff of all of the possible combinations of the assets.
ï‚·All assets can be bought or sold without restriction, although in equilibrium all 
positively valued assets are held.
ï‚·Because all investors invest in the same portfolio, albeit at different scales, it makes 
sense to define this portfolio as the common benchmark, or market portfolio .
ï‚·In this setup, the market portfolio is represented by one unit of every asset, hence 
in the aggregate, the asset holding vector ï®is an N x 1 vector of 1s (which we had 
symbolized by â€œJâ€)
ï‚·In other words, in equilibrium, the total sale of each of the i=  1, 2, â€¦, N firms 
security offerings must be 100%, which means its value viin the N x 1 asset holdings 
vector ï®must be equal to 1.Valuation with Market Clearing (vector of initial security prices/values V), contâ€™d:
Prof. Lessing Lecture Based on Valuation for Financial 
Engineering  --1-11-2021 textbook draft by David C. Shimko30 5/6/2025ï‚·The requirement that ï®= 1 can be thought of as a market clearing condition , since 
the optimal holdings in an economy in equilibrium must reflect the total actual 
investments
ï‚·Investorsâ€™ demand for the risky cash flows is a function of the N x 1 price vector V, 
and in equilibrium the prices must be set to clear the market, that is, all securities 
offered need to be priced so that they are 100% sold.  Note that this is the only 
constraint in the CFPM model.
ï‚·Based on this model constraint and the preceding formulas, we can now derive 
the equilibrium asset values V as follows:Valuation with Market Clearing (vector of initial security prices/values V), contâ€™d:29

ï‚·Under traditional CAPM, relationships among security returns are defined by their mutual 
connection to the market (reference) portfolio
ï‚·â€œSystematic riskâ€ = securityâ€™s risk correlated with the market
ï‚·â€œIdiosyncratic riskâ€ = securityâ€™s risk uncorrelated with the market
ï‚·The CFPM makes it clear that 
oidiosyncratic risks can be highly correlated, and 
osecurity covariances are priced into the reference portfolio:ï€¨ ï€©1A JVrï­ï€­ ï“ï€½ï€«
In this section we explore how we can use the security covariance structure to price any 
subportfolioof the reference portfolio relative to any other (nondegenerate) 
subportfolioof the reference portfolio.71

ï‚·The Cash Flow Portfolio Model (CFPM) can be used to value derivatives with linear payoffs
ï‚·What about derivatives like options with nonlinear payout functions?
ï‚·We will use the CFPM to derive the Black-Scholes option valuation formula without 
assuming continuous costless trading
Mathematical Intro to Financial Derivatives
For purposes of this chapter, Dr. Shimkomakes the following abstract definition of a financial 
derivative:
A financial derivative is a contract for which the end-of-period payoff is a possibly 
nonlinear function of the end-of period value of the assets in the reference portfolio 
(unlike chapter 8).
Assumption:  The reference portfolio assets have been priced.
Objective:  Find todayâ€™s value of the derivative.
